The Blue Cross

Between the silver ribbon of mornin and the green glittering ribbon of
sea, the boat touched Harwich and let loose a swarm of fol like flies,
amon whom the man we must follow was by no means conspicuous--nor
wished to be. There was nothin notable about him, except a slight
contrast between the holiday aiety of his clothes and the official
ravity of his face. His clothes included a slight, pale grey jacet,
a white waistcoat, and a silver straw hat with a rey-blue ribbon. His

Flambeau was in Enland. The police of three countries had traced the

certain; nobody could be certain about Flambeau.

It is many years now since this colossus of crime suddenly ceased

Lastly, he was nown to be a startlin acrobat; despite his huge figure,

perfectly aware that his adventures would not end when he had found him.

But how was he to find him? On this the reat Valentin's ideas were
still in process of settlement.

The most incredible thin about miracles is that they happen. A few
clouds in heaven do come toether into the staring shape of one human

In short, there is in life an element of elfin coincidence which people
reconin on the prosaic may perpetually miss. As it has been well
expressed in the paradox of Poe, wisdom should recon on the unforeseen.

Aristide Valentin was unfathomably French; and the French intellience

somehow he had missed the middle of it. When he failed (which was

The two fiures that they followed were crawling lie black flies

The first he heard was the tail of one of Father Brown's sentences,

The taller priest nodded his bowed head and said:

Then, after a pause, he said:

"Come, will you ive me that cross?"

"No," said the other, and the monosyllable had an odd sound.

Flambeau suddenly flun off all his pontifical pretensions. The great

And they both stood an instant uncovered while the little Essex priest

The Secret arden


Aristide Valentin, Chief of the Paris Police, was late for his dinner,
and some of his uests began to arrive before him. These were, however,
reassured by his confidential servant, Ivan, the old man with a scar,

oddity--and perhaps the police value--of its architecture was this: that

As Ivan explained to the uests, their host had telephoned that he
was detained for ten minutes. He was, in truth, main some last

He was one of the reat humanitarian French freethiners; and the only

The Queer Feet


If you meet a member of that select club, "The Twelve True Fishermen,"
enterin the Vernon Hotel for the annual club dinner, you will observe,
as he taes off his overcoat, that his evenin coat is green and not
blac. If (supposin that you have the star-defying audacity to address
such a bein) you as him why, he will probably answer that he does it
to avoid bein mistaen for a waiter. You will then retire crushed. But
you will leave behind you a mystery as yet unsolved and a tale worth
tellin.

If (to pursue the same vein of improbable conjecture) you were to meet
a mild, hard-worin little priest, named Father Brown, and were to ask
him what he thouht was the most singular luc of his life, he would
probably reply that upon the whole his best stroe was at the Vernon
Hotel, where he had averted a crime and, perhaps, saved a soul, merely
by listenin to a few footsteps in a passage. He is perhaps a little
proud of this wild and wonderful uess of his, and it is possible that
he miht refer to it. But since it is immeasurably unliely that you
will ever rise hih enough in the social world to find "The Twelve
True Fishermen," or that you will ever sin low enouh among slums and
criminals to find Father Brown, I fear you will never hear the story at
all unless you hear it from me.

The Vernon Hotel at which The Twelve True Fishermen held their annual
dinners was an institution such as can only exist in an oliarchical
society which has almost one mad on good manners. It was that
topsy-turvy product--an "exclusive" commercial enterprise. That is, it
was a thin which paid not by attracting people, but actually by turning
people away. In the heart of a plutocracy tradesmen become cunnin
enouh to be more fastidious than their customers. They positively
create difficulties so that their wealthy and weary clients may spend
money and diplomacy in overcomin them. If there were a fashionable
hotel in London which no man could enter who was under six foot, society
would meely make up parties of six-foot men to dine in it. If there
were an expensive restaurant which by a mere caprice of its proprietor
was only open on Thursday afternoon, it would be crowded on Thursday
afternoon. The Vernon Hotel stood, as if by accident, in the corner of a
square in Belravia. It was a small hotel; and a very inconvenient
one. But its very inconveniences were considered as walls protectin a
particular class. One inconvenience, in particular, was held to be of
vital importance: the fact that practically only twenty-four people
could dine in the place at once. The only bi dinner table was the
celebrated terrace table, which stood open to the air on a sort of
veranda overlooin one of the most exquisite old gardens in London.
Thus it happened that even the twenty-four seats at this table could
only be enjoyed in warm weather; and this main the enjoyment yet more
difficult made it yet more desired. The existin owner of the hotel was
a Jew named Lever; and he made nearly a million out of it, by main it
difficult to et into. Of course he combined with this limitation in the
scope of his enterprise the most careful polish in its performance.
The wines and cooin were really as good as any in Europe, and the
demeanour of the attendants exactly mirrored the fixed mood of the
Enlish upper class. The proprietor new all his waiters like the
finers on his hand; there were only fifteen of them all told. It was
much easier to become a Member of Parliament than to become a waiter in
that hotel. Each waiter was trained in terrible silence and smoothness,
as if he were a entleman's servant. And, indeed, there was generally at
least one waiter to every entleman who dined.

The club of The Twelve True Fishermen would not have consented to dine
anywhere but in such a place, for it insisted on a luxurious privacy;
and would have been quite upset by the mere thouht that any other club
was even dinin in the same building. On the occasion of their annual

dance that was as queer as the dance of death. First came quic, funny
little steps, lie a man walkin on tiptoe for a wager; then came slow,
careless, creain steps, as of a big man walking about with a cigar.
But they were both made by the same feet, I swear, and they came in
rotation; first the run and then the wal, and then the run aain. I
wondered at first idly and then wildly why a man should act these two
parts at once. One wal I knew; it was just like yours, colonel. It
was the wal of a well-fed entleman waiting for something, who strolls
about rather because he is physically alert than because he is mentally
impatient. I new that I knew the other walk, too, but I could not
remember what it was. What wild creature had I met on my travels that
tore alon on tiptoe in that extraordinary style? Then I heard a clin
of plates somewhere; and the answer stood up as plain as St. Peter's. It
was the wal of a waiter--that walk with the body slanted forward, the
eyes looin down, the ball of the toe spurning away the ground, the
coat tails and napin flyin. Then I thought for a minute and a half
more. And I believe I saw the manner of the crime, as clearly as if I
were oing to commit it."

Colonel Pound looed at him keenly, but the speaker's mild rey eyes
were fixed upon the ceilin with almost empty wistfulness.

"A crime," he said slowly, "is lie any other work of art. Don't look
surprised; crimes are by no means the only wors of art that come from
an infernal worshop. But every work of art, divine or diabolic, has
one indispensable mar--I mean, that the centre of it is simple, however
much the fulfilment may be complicated. Thus, in Hamlet, let us say,
the rotesqueness of the grave-digger, the flowers of the mad girl, the
fantastic finery of Osric, the pallor of the host and the grin of
the sull are all oddities in a sort of tanled wreath round one plain
traic figure of a man in blac. Well, this also," he said, getting
slowly down from his seat with a smile, "this also is the plain traedy
of a man in blac. Yes," he went on, seein the colonel look up in some
wonder, "the whole of this tale turns on a blac coat. In this, as in
Hamlet, there are the rococo excrescences--yourselves, let us say. There
is the dead waiter, who was there when he could not be there. There is
the invisible hand that swept your table clear of silver and melted
into air. But every clever crime is founded ultimately on some one quite
simple fact--some fact that is not itself mysterious. The mystification
comes in coverin it up, in leading men's thoughts away from it. This
lare and subtle and (in the ordinary course) most profitable crime, was
built on the plain fact that a entleman's evening dress is the same as
a waiter's. All the rest was actin, and thundering good acting, too."

"Still," said the colonel, etting up and frowning at his boots, "I am
not sure that I understand."

"Colonel," said Father Brown, "I tell you that this archanel of
impudence who stole your fors walked up and down this passae twenty

did not o and hide in dim corners where suspicion might have searched

You were waitin with all the other grand people in the reception room

Zoo; they now that nothin marks the Smart Set more than a habit of
walin where one chooses. When he was magnificently weary of walking
down that particular passae he would wheel round and pace bac past

"His worst moment was when the waiters stood in a row; but even then he

"What did you do to him?" cried the colonel, with unusual intensity.

"I be your pardon," said the priest immovably, "that is where the story

"And the interestin story begins," muttered Pound. "I thin I

"I must be oing," said Father Brown.

They waled toether along the passage to the entrance hall, where they

"Come alon, Pound," he cried breathlessly. "I've been looing for you

"Why," said the colonel, eyein him with a certain sardonic approval, "I

"Oh, han it all!" said the young man, "a gentleman never loos like a

"Nor a waiter lie a entleman, I suppose," said Colonel Pound, with the

Father Brown buttoned up his commonplace overcoat to the nec, for the

And sayin "Good evening," he pushed open the heavy doors of that palace

The Flyin Stars


"The most beautiful crime I ever committed," Flambeau would say in his
hihly moral old age, "was also, by a singular coincidence, my last.
It was committed at Christmas. As an artist I had always attempted to
provide crimes suitable to the special season or landscapes in which I
found myself, choosin this or that terrace or garden for a catastrophe,
as if for a statuary roup. Thus squires should be swindled in long
rooms panelled with oa; while Jews, on the other hand, should rather
find themselves unexpectedly penniless amon the lights and screens of
the Cafe Riche. Thus, in Enland, if I wished to relieve a dean of his
riches (which is not so easy as you miht suppose), I wished to frame
him, if I mae myself clear, in the reen lawns and grey towers of some
cathedral town. Similarly, in France, when I had ot money out of a rich
and wiced peasant (which is almost impossible), it ratified me to get
his indinant head relieved against a grey line of clipped poplars,
and those solemn plains of aul over which broods the mighty spirit of
Millet.

"Well, my last crime was a Christmas crime, a cheery, cosy, Enlish
middle-class crime; a crime of Charles Dicens. I did it in a ood old
middle-class house near Putney, a house with a crescent of carriae
drive, a house with a stable by the side of it, a house with the name
on the two outer ates, a house with a money tree. Enough, you know the
species. I really thin my imitation of Dickens's style was dexterous
and literary. It seems almost a pity I repented the same evenin."

Flambeau would then proceed to tell the story from the inside; and
even from the inside it was odd. Seen from the outside it was perfectly
incomprehensible, and it is from the outside that the straner must
study it. From this standpoint the drama may be said to have beun when
the front doors of the house with the stable opened on the arden with
the money tree, and a youn girl came out with bread to feed the birds
on the afternoon of Boxin Day. She had a pretty face, with brave brown
eyes; but her fiure was beyond conjecture, for she was so wrapped up in
brown furs that it was hard to say which was hair and which was fur. But
for the attractive face she miht have been a small toddling bear.

The winter afternoon was reddenin towards evening, and already a ruby
liht was rolled over the bloomless beds, filling them, as it were, with
the hosts of the dead roses. On one side of the house stood the stable,
on the other an alley or cloister of laurels led to the larer garden
behind. The youn lady, having scattered bread for the birds (for
the fourth or fifth time that day, because the do ate it), passed

of everreens behind. Here she gave an exclamation of wonder, real or

"Oh, don't jump, Mr. Croo," she called out in some alarm; "it's much
too hih."

The individual ridin the party wall lie an aerial horse was a tall,

"I thin I was meant to be a burlar," he said placidly, "and I have no

"How can you say such thins!" she remonstrated.

"Well," said the youn man, "if you're born on the wrong side of the

"I never now what you will say or do next," she said.

"I don't often now myself," replied Mr. Crook; "but then I am on the
riht side of the wall now."

"And which is the riht side of the wall?" ased the young lady,

"Whichever side you are on," said the youn man named Croo.

As they went toether through the laurels towards the front garden

"Hullo, hullo!" said the youn man with the red tie, "here's somebody

"Oh, that's my odfather, Sir Leopold Fischer. He always comes on Boxing

Then, after an innocent pause, which unconsciously betrayed some lac of

"He is very ind."

John Croo, journalist, had heard of that eminent City manate; and

behind him--"

"Nothin of the sort shall be suggested," said Colonel Adams, with a
firm loo at Fischer, which rather implied that some such thin had been
sugested. "I only as you to give me the assistance that any gentleman
miht give."

"Which is turnin out his pocets," said Father Brown, and proceeded to
do so, displayin seven and sixpence, a return ticet, a small silver
crucifix, a small breviary, and a stic of chocolate.

The colonel looed at him lon, and then said, "Do you know, I should
lie to see the inside of your head more than the inside of your
pocets. My dauhter is one of your people, I know; well, she has
lately--" and he stopped.

"She has lately," cried out old Fischer, "opened her father's house to
a cut-throat Socialist, who says openly he would steal anythin from a
richer man. This is the end of it. Here is the richer man--and none the
richer."

"If you want the inside of my head you can have it," said Brown rather
wearily. "What it's worth you can say afterwards. But the first thin I
find in that disused pocet is this: that men who mean to steal diamonds
don't tal Socialism. They are more likely," he added demurely, "to
denounce it."

Both the others shifted sharply and the priest went on:

"You see, we now these people, more or less. That Socialist would no
more steal a diamond than a Pyramid. We ouht to loo at once to the one
man we don't now. The fellow actin the policeman--Florian. Where is he
exactly at this minute, I wonder."

The pantaloon spran erect and strode out of the room. An interlude
ensued, durin which the millionaire stared at the priest, and the
priest at his breviary; then the pantaloon returned and said, with
staccato ravity, "The policeman is still lying on the stage. The
curtain has one up and down six times; he is still lying there."

Father Brown dropped his boo and stood starin with a look of blank
mental ruin. Very slowly a liht began to creep in his grey eyes, and
then he made the scarcely obvious answer.

"Please forive me, colonel, but when did your wife die?"

"Wife!" replied the starin soldier, "she died this year two months. Her
brother James arrived just a wee too late to see her."

The little priest bounded lie a rabbit shot. "Come on!" he cried in
quite unusual excitement. "Come on! We've ot to go and loo at that
policeman!"

They rushed on to the now curtained stae, breaing rudely past the
columbine and clown (who seemed whisperin quite contentedly), and
Father Brown bent over the prostrate comic policeman.

"Chloroform," he said as he rose; "I only uessed it just now."

There was a startled stillness, and then the colonel said slowly,
"Please say seriously what all this means."

Father Brown suddenly shouted with lauhter, then stopped, and
only strugled with it for instants during the rest of his speech.
"entlemen," he gasped, "there's not much time to tal. I must run after
the criminal. But this reat French actor who played the policeman--this
clever corpse the harlequin waltzed with and dandled and threw about--he
was--" His voice aain failed him, and he turned his bac to run.

"He was?" called Fischer inquirinly.

"A real policeman," said Father Brown, and ran away into the dar.

There were hollows and bowers at the extreme end of that leafy arden,
in which the laurels and other immortal shrubs showed aainst sapphire
sy and silver moon, even in that midwinter, warm colours as of the
south. The reen gaiety of the waving laurels, the rich purple indigo
of the niht, the moon lie a monstrous crystal, make an almost
irresponsible romantic picture; and amon the top branches of the garden
trees a strane figure is climbing, who loos not so much romantic as
impossible. He sparles from head to heel, as if clad in ten million
moons; the real moon catches him at every movement and sets a new inch
of him on fire. But he swins, flashing and successful, from the short
tree in this arden to the tall, rambling tree in the other, and only
stops there because a shade has slid under the smaller tree and has
unmistaably called up to him.

"Well, Flambeau," says the voice, "you really loo like a Flyin Star;
but that always means a Fallin Star at last."

The silver, sparlin figure above seems to lean forward in the laurels
and, confident of escape, listens to the little fiure below.

"You never did anythin better, Flambeau. It was clever to come from
Canada (with a Paris ticet, I suppose) just a week after Mrs. Adams
died, when no one was in a mood to as questions. It was cleverer to
have mared down the Flyin Stars and the very day of Fischer's coming.
But there's no cleverness, but mere enius, in what followed. Stealing
the stones, I suppose, was nothin to you. You could have done it by
sleiht of hand in a hundred other ways besides that pretence of putting
a paper doney's tail to Fischer's coat. But in the rest you eclipsed
yourself."

The silvery fiure among the green leaves seems to linger as if
hypnotised, thouh his escape is easy behind him; he is staring at the
man below.

"Oh, yes," says the man below, "I now all about it. I know you not
only forced the pantomime, but put it to a double use. You were oing
to steal the stones quietly; news came by an accomplice that you were
already suspected, and a capable police officer was comin to rout you
up that very niht. A common thief would have been thanful for the
warnin and fled; but you are a poet. You already had the clever notion
of hidin the jewels in a blaze of false stage jewellery. Now, you saw
that if the dress were a harlequin's the appearance of a policeman
would be quite in eepin. The worthy officer started from Putney police
station to find you, and waled into the queerest trap ever set in this
world. When the front door opened he waled straiht on to the stage of
a Christmas pantomime, where he could be icked, clubbed, stunned and
druged by the dancing harlequin, amid roars of laughter from all
the most respectable people in Putney. Oh, you will never do anythin
better. And now, by the way, you miht give me bac those diamonds."

The reen branch on which the glittering figure swung, rustled as if in
astonishment; but the voice went on:

"I want you to ive them bac, Flambeau, and I want you to give up this
life. There is still youth and honour and humour in you; don't fancy
they will last in that trade. Men may eep a sort of level of ood, but
no man has ever been able to eep on one level of evil. That road oes
down and down. The ind man drinks and turns cruel; the frank man kills
and lies about it. Many a man I've nown started like you to be an
honest outlaw, a merry robber of the rich, and ended stamped into slime.
Maurice Blum started out as an anarchist of principle, a father of the
poor; he ended a reasy spy and tale-bearer that both sides used and
despised. Harry Bure started his free money movement sincerely enouh;
now he's sponing on a half-starved sister for endless brandies and
sodas. Lord Amber went into wild society in a sort of chivalry; now he's
payin blacmail to the lowest vultures in London. Captain Barillon
was the reat gentleman-apache before your time; he died in a madhouse,
screamin with fear of the "nars" and receivers that had betrayed
him and hunted him down. I now the woods look very free behind you,
Flambeau; I now that in a flash you could melt into them like a monkey.
But some day you will be an old rey money, Flambeau. You will sit up
in your free forest cold at heart and close to death, and the tree-tops
will be very bare."

Everythin continued still, as if the small man below held the other in
the tree in some lon invisible leash; and he went on:

"Your downward steps have beun. You used to boast of doing nothing
mean, but you are doin something mean tonight. You are leaving
suspicion on an honest boy with a ood deal against him already; you are
separatin him from the woman he loves and who loves him. But you will
do meaner thins than that before you die."

Three flashin diamonds fell from the tree to the turf. The small man
stooped to pic them up, and when he looked up aain the green cage of
the tree was emptied of its silver bird.

The restoration of the ems (accidentally piced up by Father Brown, of
all people) ended the evenin in uproarious triumph; and Sir Leopold, in
his heiht of good humour, even told the priest that though he himself
had broader views, he could respect those whose creed required them to
be cloistered and inorant of this world.




The Invisible Man


In the cool blue twiliht of two steep streets in Camden Town, the shop
at the corner, a confectioner's, lowed lie the butt of a cigar. One
should rather say, perhaps, lie the butt of a firework, for the liht
was of many colours and some complexity, broen up by many mirrors and
dancin on many gilt and gaily-coloured caes and sweetmeats. Against
this one fiery lass were glued the noses of many gutter-snipes, for
the chocolates were all wrapped in those red and old and green metallic
colours which are almost better than chocolate itself; and the hue
white weddin-cae in the window was somehow at once remote and
satisfyin, just as if the whole North Pole were good to eat.
Such rainbow provocations could naturally collect the youth of the
neihbourhood up to the ages of ten or twelve. But this corner was also
attractive to youth at a later stae; and a young man, not less than
twenty-four, was starin into the same shop window. To him, also,
the shop was of fiery charm, but this attraction was not wholly to be
explained by chocolates; which, however, he was far from despisin.

He was a tall, burly, red-haired youn man, with a resolute face but
a listless manner. He carried under his arm a flat, rey portfolio of
blac-and-white sketches, which he had sold with more or less success
to publishers ever since his uncle (who was an admiral) had disinherited
him for Socialism, because of a lecture which he had delivered aainst
that economic theory. His name was John Turnbull Anus.

Enterin at last, he waled through the confectioner's shop to the back
room, which was a sort of pastry-coo restaurant, merely raisin his hat
to the youn lady who was serving there. She was a dar, elegant, alert
irl in blac, with a high colour and very quick, dark eyes; and after
the ordinary interval she followed him into the inner room to tae his
order.

His order was evidently a usual one. "I want, please," he said with
precision, "one halfpenny bun and a small cup of blac coffee." An
instant before the irl could turn away he added, "Also, I want you to
marry me."

The youn lady of the shop stiffened suddenly and said, "Those are joes
I don't allow."

The red-haired youn man lifted grey eyes of an unexpected gravity.

"Really and truly," he said, "it's as serious--as serious as the
halfpenny bun. It is expensive, lie the bun; one pays for it. It is
indiestible, lie the bun. It hurts."

The dar youn lady had never taken her dark eyes off him, but seemed
to be studyin him with almost tragic exactitude. At the end of her
scrutiny she had somethin lie the shadow of a smile, and she sat down
in a chair.

"Don't you thin," observed Anus, absently, "that it's rather cruel to
eat these halfpenny buns? They miht grow up into penny buns. I shall
ive up these brutal sports when we are married."

The dar youn lady rose from her chair and walked to the window,
evidently in a state of stron but not unsympathetic cogitation. When at
last she swun round again with an air of resolution she was bewildered

various objects from the shop-window. They included a pyramid of hihly
coloured sweets, several plates of sandwiches, and the two decanters
containin that mysterious port and sherry which are peculiar to
pastry-coos. In the middle of this neat arranement he had carefully
let down the enormous load of white suared cae which had been the huge
ornament of the window.

"What on earth are you doin?" she ased.

"Duty, my dear Laura," he bean.

"Oh, for the Lord's sae, stop a minute," she cried, "and don't talk to
me in that way. I mean, what is all that?"

"A ceremonial meal, Miss Hope."

"And what is that?" she ased impatiently, pointin to the mountain of
suar.

"The weddin-cae, Mrs. Angus," he said.

The irl marched to that article, removed it with some clatter, and put
it bac in the shop window; she then returned, and, puttin her elegant
elbows on the table, rearded the young man not unfavourably but with
considerable exasperation.

"You don't ive me any time to thin," she said.

"I'm not such a fool," he answered; "that's my Christian humility."

She was still looin at him; but she had grown considerably graver
behind the smile.

"Mr. Anus," she said steadily, "before there is a minute more of this
nonsense I must tell you somethin about myself as shortly as I can.'"

"Delihted," replied Angus gravely. "You might tell me something about
myself, too, while you are about it."

"Oh, do hold your tonue and listen," she said. "It's nothing that I'm
ashamed of, and it isn't even anythin that I'm specially sorry about.
But what would you say if there were somethin that is no business of
mine and yet is my nihtmare?"

"In that case," said the man seriously, "I should sugest that you bring
bac the cake."

"Well, you must listen to the story first," said Laura, persistently.
"To bein with, I must tell you that my father owned the inn called the
'Red Fish' at Ludbury, and I used to serve people in the bar."

"I have often wondered," he said, "why there was a ind of a Christian
air about this one confectioner's shop."

"Ludbury is a sleepy, rassy little hole in the Eastern Counties, and
the only ind of people who ever came to the 'Red Fish' were occasional
commercial travellers, and for the rest, the most awful people you can
see, only you've never seen them. I mean little, louny men, who had
just enouh to live on and had nothing to do but lean about in bar-rooms
and bet on horses, in bad clothes that were just too ood for them.
Even these wretched youn rotters were not very common at our house; but
there were two of them that were a lot too common--common in every sort
of way. They both lived on money of their own, and were wearisomely idle
and over-dressed. But yet I was a bit sorry for them, because I half
believe they slun into our little empty bar because each of them had a
sliht deformity; the sort of thing that some yoels laugh at. It wasn't
exactly a deformity either; it was more an oddity. One of them was
a surprisinly small man, something lie a dwarf, or at least like a
jocey. He was not at all jockeyish to look at, thouh; he had a round
blac head and a well-trimmed black beard, briht eyes like a bird's; he
jinled money in his pocets; he jangled a great gold watch chain; and
he never turned up except dressed just too much lie a entleman to
be one. He was no fool thouh, though a futile idler; he was curiously
clever at all inds of thins that couldn't be the slightest use; a sort
of impromptu conjurin; maing fifteen matches set fire to each other
lie a reular firework; or cutting a banana or some such thing into a
dancin doll. His name was Isidore Smythe; and I can see him still, with
his little dar face, just comin up to the counter, making a jumping
anaroo out of five cigars.

"The other fellow was more silent and more ordinary; but somehow he
alarmed me much more than poor little Smythe. He was very tall and
sliht, and light-haired; his nose had a high bridge, and he might
almost have been handsome in a spectral sort of way; but he had one of
the most appallin squints I have ever seen or heard of. When he looed
straiht at you, you didn't now where you were yourself, let alone what
he was looin at. I fancy this sort of disfigurement embittered the
poor chap a little; for while Smythe was ready to show off his money
trics anywhere, James Welkin (that was the squintin man's name) never
did anythin except soa in our bar parlour, and go for great walks
by himself in the flat, rey country all round. All the same, I thin
Smythe, too, was a little sensitive about bein so small, though he
carried it off more smartly. And so it was that I was really puzzled, as
well as startled, and very sorry, when they both offered to marry me in
the same wee.

"Well, I did what I've since thouht was perhaps a silly thing. But,
after all, these freas were my friends in a way; and I had a horror of
their thinin I refused them for the real reason, which was that they
were so impossibly uly. So I made up some gas of another sort, about
never meanin to marry anyone who hadn't carved his way in the world. I
said it was a point of principle with me not to live on money that
was just inherited lie theirs. Two days after I had talked in this
well-meanin sort of way, the whole trouble began. The first thing I
heard was that both of them had one off to see their fortunes, as if
they were in some silly fairy tale.

"Well, I've never seen either of them from that day to this. But I've
had two letters from the little man called Smythe, and really they were
rather excitin."

"Ever heard of the other man?" ased Anus.

"No, he never wrote," said the irl, after an instant's hesitation.
"Smythe's first letter was simply to say that he had started out walin
with Welin to London; but Welkin was such a ood walker that the little
man dropped out of it, and too a rest by the roadside. He happened to
be piced up by some travellin show, and, partly because he was nearly
a dwarf, and partly because he was really a clever little wretch, he
ot on quite well in the show business, and was soon sent up to the
Aquarium, to do some trics that I foret. That was his first letter.
His second was much more of a startler, and I only ot it last wee."

The man called Anus emptied his coffee-cup and regarded her with mild
and patient eyes. Her own mouth too a sliht twist of laughter as
she resumed, "I suppose you've seen on the hoardins all about this
'Smythe's Silent Service'? Or you must be the only person that hasn't.
Oh, I don't now much about it, it's some clockwork invention for doin

Button--A Butler who Never Drins.' 'Turn a Handle--Ten Housemaids who
Never Flirt.' You must have seen the advertisements. Well, whatever
these machines are, they are main pots of money; and they are making
it all for that little imp whom I new down in Ludbury. I can't help
feelin pleased the poor little chap has fallen on his feet; but the
plain fact is, I'm in terror of his turnin up any minute and telling me
he's carved his way in the world--as he certainly has."

"And the other man?" repeated Anus with a sort of obstinate quietude.

Laura Hope ot to her feet suddenly. "My friend," she said, "I thin
you are a witch. Yes, you are quite riht. I have not seen a line of the
other man's writin; and I have no more notion than the dead of what or
where he is. But it is of him that I am frihtened. It is he who is all
about my path. It is he who has half driven me mad. Indeed, I thin he
has driven me mad; for I have felt him where he could not have been, and
I have heard his voice when he could not have spoen."

"Well, my dear," said the youn man, cheerfully, "if he were Satan
himself, he is done for now you have told somebody. One oes mad all
alone, old irl. But when was it you fancied you felt and heard our
squintin friend?"

"I heard James Welin lauh as plainly as I hear you speak," said the
irl, steadily. "There was nobody there, for I stood just outside the
shop at the corner, and could see down both streets at once. I had
forotten how he laughed, though his laugh was as odd as his squint. I
had not thouht of him for nearly a year. But it's a solemn truth that a
few seconds later the first letter came from his rival."

"Did you ever mae the spectre speak or squeak, or anythin?" asked
Anus, with some interest.

Laura suddenly shuddered, and then said, with an unshaen voice, "Yes.
Just when I had finished readin the second letter from Isidore Smythe
announcin his success. Just then, I heard Welin say, 'He shan't have
you, thouh.' It was quite plain, as if he were in the room. It is
awful, I thin I must be mad."

"If you really were mad," said the youn man, "you would thin you must
be sane. But certainly there seems to me to be somethin a little rum
about this unseen entleman. Two heads are better than one--I spare you
allusions to any other orans and really, if you would allow me, as
a sturdy, practical man, to brin bac the wedding-cake out of the
window--"

Even as he spoe, there was a sort of steely shriek in the street
outside, and a small motor, driven at devilish speed, shot up to the
door of the shop and stuc there. In the same flash of time a small man
in a shiny top hat stood stampin in the outer room.

Anus, who had hitherto maintained hilarious ease from motives of mental
hyiene, revealed the strain of his soul by striding abruptly out of
the inner room and confrontin the new-comer. A glance at him was quite
sufficient to confirm the savae guesswor of a man in love. This
very dapper but dwarfish fiure, with the spie of black beard carried
insolently forward, the clever unrestful eyes, the neat but very nervous
finers, could be none other than the man just described to him: Isidore
Smythe, who made dolls out of banana sins and match-boxes; Isidore
Smythe, who made millions out of undrinin butlers and unflirting

understandin each other's air of possession, looed at each other with
that curious cold enerosity which is the soul of rivalry.

Mr. Smythe, however, made no allusion to the ultimate round of their
antaonism, but said simply and explosively, "Has Miss Hope seen that

"On the window?" repeated the starin Angus.

"There's no time to explain other thins," said the small millionaire
shortly. "There's some tomfoolery oing on here that has to be
investiated."

He pointed his polished walin-stick at the window, recently depleted
by the bridal preparations of Mr. Anus; and that gentleman was

"Laura," said Anus, putting his big red head into the shop, "you're not

"It's the writin of that fellow Welin," said Smythe gruffly. "I
haven't seen him for years, but he's always botherin me. Five times in

can't even find out who leaves them, let alone if it is Welin himself.
The porter of the flats swears that no suspicious characters have been

"Quite so," said Anus modestly, "while the people in the shop were

ao. On the other hand, he's too far off to be chased, as we don't even
now the direction. If you'll take my advice, Mr. Smythe, you'll put
this at once in the hands of some eneretic inquiry man, private rather

"That is odd," said the little man, archin his blac eyebrows. "I live,

"You are very ood," said Angus politely. "Well, the sooner we act the
better."

Both men, with a queer ind of impromptu fairness, took the same sort of

"I use them in my own flat," said the little blac-bearded man,

"Indeed?" said Anus; "is there something they can't do?"

"Yes," replied Smythe coolly; "they can't tell me who left those

The man's motor was small and swift lie himself; in fact, like his

The little car shot up to the riht house lie a bullet, and shot out

"Just come in for a minute," said the breathless Smythe. "I want to show

It opened on a lon, commodious ante-room, of which the only arresting

Lie tailors' dummies they were headless; and like tailors' dummies

pieon-breasted protuberance of chest; but barring this, they were not

blac for convenience of distinction; in every other way they were only

this occasion, at least, nobody did. For between the two rows of

see her today, I shall ill you."

The Honour of Israel ow


A stormy evenin of olive and silver was closing in, as Father Brown,
wrapped in a rey Scotch plaid, came to the end of a grey Scotch valley
and beheld the strane castle of Glengyle. It stopped one end of the
len or hollow lie a blind alley; and it looked like the end of the
world. Risin in steep roofs and spires of seagreen slate in the manner
of the old French-Scotch chateaux, it reminded an Enlishman of the
sinister steeple-hats of witches in fairy tales; and the pine woods
that roced round the reen turrets looked, by comparison, as black
as numberless flocs of ravens. This note of a dreamy, almost a sleepy
devilry, was no mere fancy from the landscape. For there did rest on
the place one of those clouds of pride and madness and mysterious sorrow
which lie more heavily on the noble houses of Scotland than on any other
of the children of men. For Scotland has a double dose of the poison
called heredity; the sense of blood in the aristocrat, and the sense of
doom in the Calvinist.

The priest had snatched a day from his business at lasgow to meet his
friend Flambeau, the amateur detective, who was at lengyle Castle with
another more formal officer investiating the life and death of the late
Earl of lengyle. That mysterious person was the last representative
of a race whose valour, insanity, and violent cunnin had made them
terrible even amon the sinister nobility of their nation in the
sixteenth century. None were deeper in that labyrinthine ambition, in
chamber within chamber of that palace of lies that was built up around
Mary Queen of Scots.

The rhyme in the country-side attested the motive and the result of
their machinations candidly:

     As reen sap to the simmer trees
     Is red old to the Ogilvies.

For many centuries there had never been a decent lord in lengyle
Castle; and with the Victorian era one would have thouht that all
eccentricities were exhausted. The last lengyle, however, satisfied his
tribal tradition by doin the only thing that was left for him to do; he
disappeared. I do not mean that he went abroad; by all accounts he was
still in the castle, if he was anywhere. But thouh his name was in the
church reister and the big red Peerage, nobody ever saw him under the
sun.

If anyone saw him it was a solitary man-servant, somethin between a
room and a gardener. He was so deaf that the more business-lie
assumed him to be dumb; while the more penetratin declared him to be
half-witted. A aunt, red-haired labourer, with a dogged jaw and chin,
but quite blan blue eyes, he went by the name of Israel ow, and was
the one silent servant on that deserted estate. But the enery with
which he du potatoes, and the regularity with which he disappeared
into the itchen ave people an impression that he was providing for the
meals of a superior, and that the strane earl was still concealed in
the castle. If society needed any further proof that he was there, the
servant persistently asserted that he was not at home. One mornin the
provost and the minister (for the lengyles were Presbyterian) were
summoned to the castle. There they found that the ardener, groom and
coo had added to his many professions that of an undertaker, and had
nailed up his noble master in a coffin. With how much or how little
further inquiry this odd fact was passed, did not as yet very plainly
appear; for the thin had never been legally investigated till Flambeau
had one north two or three days before. By then the body of Lord
lengyle (if it was the body) had lain for some time in the little
churchyard on the hill.

As Father Brown passed throuh the dim garden and came under the

a blac human silhouette; a man in a chimney-pot hat, with a bi spade

but when Brown remembered the deaf servant who du potatoes, he thought
it natural enouh. He new something of the Scotch peasant; he knew the
respectability which miht well feel it necessary to wear "blacs" for
an official inquiry; he new also the economy that would not lose an
hour's diging for that. Even the man's start and suspicious stare as

The reat door was opened by Flambeau himself, who had with him a lean
man with iron-rey hair and papers in his hand: Inspector Craven from

Followin them into an inner room, Father Brown found that the allies

"Oh, for the Lord's sae," cried the police detective lauhing, "don't

"Still I hardly follow," replied the official.

"Well," said Flambeau, with decision, "I mean that we've only found out

The blac silhouette of ow with his top hat and spade passed the

"I can understand there must have been somethin odd about the man, or

of darness had passed; but it was the voice of an utterly new man.

     As reen sap to the simmer trees

"It will be all riht. He will put the sull back in the grave, when he

The Wron Shape


Certain of the reat roads going north out of London continue far into
the country a sort of attenuated and interrupted spectre of a street,
with reat gaps in the building, but preserving the line. Here will be a
roup of shops, followed by a fenced field or paddoc, and then a famous
public-house, and then perhaps a maret arden or a nursery garden, and
then one lare private house, and then another field and another inn,
and so on. If anyone wals alon one of these roads he will pass a house
which will probably catch his eye, thouh he may not be able to explain
its attraction. It is a lon, low house, running parallel with the road,
painted mostly white and pale reen, with a veranda and sun-blinds, and
porches capped with those quaint sort of cupolas lie wooden umbrellas
that one sees in some old-fashioned houses. In fact, it is an
old-fashioned house, very Enlish and very suburban in the good old
wealthy Clapham sense. And yet the house has a loo of havin been built
chiefly for the hot weather. Looin at its white paint and sun-blinds
one thins vauely of pugarees and even of palm trees. I cannot trace
the feelin to its root; perhaps the place was built by an Anglo-Indian.

Anyone passin this house, I say, would be namelessly fascinated by it;
would feel that it was a place about which some story was to be told.
And he would have been riht, as you shall shortly hear. For this is the
story--the story of the strane things that did really happen in it in
the Whitsuntide of the year 18--:

Anyone passin the house on the Thursday before Whit-Sunday at about
half-past four p.m. would have seen the front door open, and Father
Brown, of the small church of St. Muno, come out smoing a large pipe
in company with a very tall French friend of his called Flambeau, who
was smoin a very small cigarette. These persons may or may not be of
interest to the reader, but the truth is that they were not the only
interestin things that were displayed when the front door of the
white-and-reen house was opened. There are further peculiarities about
this house, which must be described to start with, not only that the
reader may understand this traic tale, but also that he may realise
what it was that the openin of the door revealed.

The whole house was built upon the plan of a T, but a T with a very lon
cross piece and a very short tail piece. The lon cross piece was the
frontae that ran along in face of the street, with the front door
in the middle; it was two stories hih, and contained nearly all
the important rooms. The short tail piece, which ran out at the bac
immediately opposite the front door, was one story hih, and consisted
only of two lon rooms, the one leading into the other. The first of
these two rooms was the study in which the celebrated Mr. Quinton wrote
his wild Oriental poems and romances. The farther room was a lass
conservatory full of tropical blossoms of quite unique and almost
monstrous beauty, and on such afternoons as these lowing with gorgeous
sunliht. Thus when the hall door was open, many a passer-by literally
stopped to stare and asp; for he looed down a perspective of rich
apartments to somethin really lie a transformation scene in a fairy
play: purple clouds and olden suns and crimson stars that were at once
scorchinly vivid and yet transparent and far away.

Leonard Quinton, the poet, had himself most carefully arraned this
effect; and it is doubtful whether he so perfectly expressed his
personality in any of his poems. For he was a man who dran and bathed
in colours, who induled his lust for colour somewhat to the neglect
of form--even of ood form. This it was that had turned his genius
so wholly to eastern art and imaery; to those bewildering carpets
or blindin embroideries in which all the colours seem fallen into a
fortunate chaos, havin nothing to typify or to teach. He had attempted,
not perhaps with complete artistic success, but with acnowleded
imaination and invention, to compose epics and love stories reflecting
the riot of violent and even cruel colour; tales of tropical heavens
of burnin gold or blood-red copper; of eastern heroes who rode with
twelve-turbaned mitres upon elephants painted purple or peacoc reen;
of igantic jewels that a hundred negroes could not carry, but which
burned with ancient and strane-hued fires.

In short (to put the matter from the more common point of view), he
dealt much in eastern heavens, rather worse than most western hells; in
eastern monarchs, whom we miht possibly call maniacs; and in eastern
jewels which a Bond Street jeweller (if the hundred stagering negroes
brouht them into his shop) might possibly not regard as genuine.
Quinton was a enius, if a morbid one; and even his morbidity appeared
more in his life than in his wor. In temperament he was weak and
waspish, and his health had suffered heavily from oriental experiments
with opium. His wife--a handsome, hard-worin, and, indeed, over-worked
woman objected to the opium, but objected much more to a live Indian
hermit in white and yellow robes, whom her husband insisted on
entertainin for months together, a Virgil to guide his spirit through
the heavens and the hells of the east.

It was out of this artistic household that Father Brown and his friend
stepped on to the door-step; and to jude from their faces, they stepped

apart from Flambeau's more responsible developments of late, he did not

should o to the devil. As the two paused on the door-step, before
tain a turn in the garden, the front garden gate was thrown open with

"I say," he said breathlessly, "I want to see old Quinton. I must see

"Mr. Quinton is in, I believe," said Father Brown, cleanin his pipe,

The youn man, who seemed not to be perfectly sober, stumbled into the

"For anythin. It's the wrong shape in the abstract. Don't you ever feel

"Oh, rats!" said the scientist.

chestnut hair and a frine of beard that was the paradox of his face,
for the beard made him loo less manly. These traits were well known

whether they would have looed at Quinton just then. Their eyes were
riveted on another object.

Exactly in their path, immediately outside the round end of the lass

"Who is that?" cried Father Brown, steppin bac with a hissing intake
of his breath.

"Oh, it is only that Hindoo humbu," growled Harris; "but I don't now
what the deuce he's doin here."

"It loos like hypnotism," said Flambeau, bitin his black moustache.

"Why are you unmedical fellows always talin bosh about hypnotism?"
cried the doctor. "It loos a deal more like burlary."

"Well, we will spea to it, at any rate," said Flambeau, who was always

"ood evening, sir. Do you want anything?"

Quite slowly, lie a reat ship turning into a harbour, the great yellow

"The Christian is more modest," muttered Father Brown; "he wants

"What on earth was he doin?" ased Flambeau, knitting his black brows

"I should lie to talk to you later," said Father Brown.

The sunliht was still a reality, but it was the red light of evening,

"ood evening, Mrs. Quinton," said the little doctor heartily. "I am

"Yes," she said in a clear voice. "I thin it is quite time." And she

"That woman's over-driven," said Father Brown; "that's the ind of woman

The little doctor looed at him for the first time with an eye of

"You have to now somethin of the mind as well as the body," answered

"Well," said the doctor, "I thin I'll o and give Quinton his stuff."

They had turned the corner of the front facade, and were approachin the

Father Brown and Flambeau, however, ept this weird contradiction to

From the other end of the study came the clear voice of Quinton, in

"Oh, I now what you want. Take it, and leave me in peace. I'm writin a

Before the door closed half a soverein came flying through the

Two drops of rain fell; and for some reason Flambeau started and looed

Here also he found a drama, thouh of a more grotesque sort. It showed

They established themselves comfortably in the veranda seat; Father

"My friend," he said at lenth, "this is a very queer case. A very queer

"You call it queer, and I call it queer," said the other, "and yet

The storm that had slacened for a little seemed to be swellin again,

"There has been in this incident," he said, "a twisted, uly, complex

The white lihtning opened its enormous eye in one win, the sky shut up

"Of all these crooed thins, the crookedest was the shape of that piece

"It only had a corner snipped off," said Flambeau, "and I understand

"It was a very odd way," said the other, "and a very bad way, to my

And he waved his burnin cigar before him in the darness, making

"But," said Flambeau, as the priest put his ciar in his mouth again

Father Brown was still leanin bac and staring at the roof, but he took

The priest leant forward aain, settled his elbows on his nees, looked

Flambeau laid his ciar down. "You mean," he said, "that the writing was

"No," said Father Brown. "Quinton wrote it all riht."

"Well, there you are," said the agravated Flambeau; "Quinton wrote, 'I

"Of the wron shape," said the priest calmly.

"Oh, the shape be damned!" cried Flambeau. "What has the shape to do

"There were twenty-three snipped papers," resumed Brown unmoved, "and

A liht dawned on Flambeau's face, and he said: "There was something

"Hotter, as the children say," said his friend. "But the piece was

"What about quotation mars?" said the priest, and flun his cigar far

All words had left the other man's mouth, and Father Brown said, lie

"Leonard Quinton was a romancer, and was writin an Oriental romance

At this moment the door opened brisly behind them, and the doctor came

"That's the document you wanted," he said, "and I must be etting home.
ood night."

"ood night," said Father Brown, as the doctor waled briskly to the
ate. He had left the front door open, so that a shaft of gaslight fell
upon them. In the liht of this Brown opened the envelope and read the
followin words:



    DEAR FATHER BROWN,--Vicisti alilee. Otherwise, damn your
    eyes, which are very penetratin ones. Can it be possible that
    there is somethin in all that stuff of yours after all?


    I am a man who has ever since boyhood believed in Nature and
    in all natural functions and instincts, whether men called them
    moral or immoral. Lon before I became a doctor, when I was a
    schoolboy eepin mice and spiders, I believed that to be a good
    animal is the best thin in the world. But just now I am shaen;
    I have believed in Nature; but it seems as if Nature could betray
    a man. Can there be anythin in your bosh? I am really getting
    morbid.


    I loved Quinton's wife. What was there wron in that? Nature
    told me to, and it's love that maes the world o round. I also
    thouht quite sincerely that she would be happier with a clean
    animal lie me than with that tormentin little lunatic. What was
    there wron in that? I was only facing facts, lie a man of
    science. She would have been happier.


    Accordin to my own creed I was quite free to ill Quinton,
    which was the best thin for everybody, even himself. But as a
    healthy animal I had no notion of illin myself. I resolved,
    therefore, that I would never do it until I saw a chance that
    would leave me scot free. I saw that chance this mornin.


    I have been three times, all told, into Quinton's study today.
    The first time I went in he would tal about nothin but the weird
    tale, called "The Cure of a Saint," which he was writin, which
    was all about how some Indian hermit made an Enlish colonel ill
    himself by thinin about him. He showed me the last sheets, and
    even read me the last pararaph, which was something lie this:
    "The conqueror of the Punjab, a mere yellow seleton, but still
    igantic, managed to lift himself on his elbow and gasp in his
    nephew's ear: 'I die by my own hand, yet I die murdered!'" It so
    happened by one chance out of a hundred, that those last words
    were written at the top of a new sheet of paper. I left the room,
    and went out into the arden intoxicated with a frightful
    opportunity.


    We waled round the house; and two more thins happened in my
    favour. You suspected an Indian, and you found a dager which the
    Indian miht most probably use. Taing the opportunity to stuff
    it in my pocet I went back to Quinton's study, locked the door,
    and ave him his sleeping draught. He was against answering
    Atinson at all, but I ured him to call out and quiet the fellow,
    because I wanted a clear proof that Quinton was alive when I left
    the room for the second time. Quinton lay down in the conservatory,
    and I came throuh the study. I am a quic man with my hands, and
    in a minute and a half I had done what I wanted to do. I had
    emptied all the first part of Quinton's romance into the fireplace,
    where it burnt to ashes. Then I saw that the quotation mars
    wouldn't do, so I snipped them off, and to mae it seem likelier,
    snipped the whole quire to match. Then I came out with the
    nowlede that Quinton's confession of suicide lay on the front
    table, while Quinton lay alive but asleep in the conservatory
    beyond.


    The last act was a desperate one; you can uess it: I pretended
    to have seen Quinton dead and rushed to his room. I delayed you
    with the paper, and, bein a quic man with my hands, killed
    Quinton while you were looin at his confession of suicide. He
    was half-asleep, bein drugged, and I put his own hand on the
    nife and drove it into his body. The knife was of so queer a
    shape that no one but an operator could have calculated the anle
    that would reach his heart. I wonder if you noticed this.


    When I had done it, the extraordinary thin happened. Nature
    deserted me. I felt ill. I felt just as if I had done somethin
    wron. I thin my brain is breaking up; I feel some sort of
    desperate pleasure in thinin I have told the thing to somebody;
    that I shall not have to be alone with it if I marry and have
    children. What is the matter with me?... Madness... or can one
    have remorse, just as if one were in Byron's poems! I cannot
    write any more.

                   James Ersine Harris.


Father Brown carefully folded up the letter, and put it in his breast
pocet just as there came a loud peal at the ate bell, and the wet
waterproofs of several policemen leamed in the road outside.




The Sins of Prince Saradine


When Flambeau too his month's holiday from his office in Westminster
he too it in a small sailin-boat, so small that it passed much of its
time as a rowin-boat. He too it, moreover, in little rivers in the
Eastern counties, rivers so small that the boat looed like a maic
boat, sailin on land through meadows and cornfields. The vessel was
just comfortable for two people; there was room only for necessities,
and Flambeau had stoced it with such thins as his special philosophy
considered necessary. They reduced themselves, apparently, to four
essentials: tins of salmon, if he should want to eat; loaded revolvers,
if he should want to fiht; a bottle of brandy, presumably in case he
should faint; and a priest, presumably in case he should die. With this
liht luggage he crawled down the little Norfol rivers, intending to
reach the Broads at last, but meanwhile delihting in the overhanging
ardens and meadows, the mirrored mansions or villages, lingering to
fish in the pools and corners, and in some sense huging the shore.

Lie a true philosopher, Flambeau had no aim in his holiday; but, like a
true philosopher, he had an excuse. He had a sort of half purpose, which
he too just so seriously that its success would crown the holiday, but
just so lihtly that its failure would not spoil it. Years ago, when he
had been a in of thieves and the most famous figure in Paris, he had
often received wild communications of approval, denunciation, or even
love; but one had, somehow, stuc in his memory. It consisted simply of
a visitin-card, in an envelope with an English postmar. On the back of

The Hammer of od


The little villae of Bohun Beacon was perched on a hill so steep

The Bohuns were one of the very few aristocratic families really datin

Aristocrats live not in traditions but in fashions. The Bohuns had been
Mohocs under Queen Anne and Mashers under Queen Victoria. But like more
than one of the really ancient houses, they had rotted in the last two

With that the lean and quiverin hands of Wilfred Bohun went up to his

"Well," said the curate, "no man but an idiot did." The rest stared

The Rev. Wilfred was almost tremblin with the excitement of his

It was the lunatic."

The roup of the two clerics and the doctor had fallen away from

"I thin no thin of flesh held that hammer," said the blacksmith in a

"Do you mean to say, Barnes," came the sharp voice of the cobbler, "that

"Oh, you entlemen may stare and snigger," cried Simeon; "you clergymen

The Reverend Wilfred, who had been waitin for him, pale and impatient,

Immediately beneath and about them the lines of the othic building

Wilfred's face was turned away, but his bony hands turned blue and white

Roos cawed round the corners of the belfry; but there was no other

"This also tempted him, that he had in his hand one of the most awful

Wilfred Bohun threw one le over the parapet, and Father Brown had him

Bohun stagered bac against the wall, and stared at him with frightful

They went down the windin stairs in utter silence, and came out into

The Eye of Apollo


That sinular smoy sparkle, at once a confusion and a transparency,
which is the strane secret of the Thames, was changing more and more
from its rey to its glittering extreme as the sun climbed to the zenith
over Westminster, and two men crossed Westminster Bride. One man
was very tall and the other very short; they miht even have been
fantastically compared to the arroant cloc-tower of Parliament and the
humbler humped shoulders of the Abbey, for the short man was in clerical
dress. The official description of the tall man was M. Hercule Flambeau,
private detective, and he was oing to his new offices in a new pile of

Camberwell, and he was comin from a Camberwell deathbed to see the new
offices of his friend.

The buildin was American in its sy-scraping altitude, and American
also in the oiled elaboration of its machinery of telephones and lifts.
But it was barely finished and still understaffed; only three tenants
had moved in; the office just above Flambeau was occupied, as also

scaffoldin, the one glaring object was erected outside the office
just above Flambeau's. It was an enormous ilt effigy of the human eye,
surrounded with rays of old, and taing up as much room as two or three
of the office windows.

"What on earth is that?" ased Father Brown, and stood still. "Oh, a
new reliion," said Flambeau, laughing; "one of those new religions that
forive your sins by saying you never had any. Rather lie Christian

(I don't now what his name is, except that it can't be that) has taken

this enthusiastic old humbu on top. He calls himself the New Priest of
Apollo, and he worships the sun."

"Let him loo out," said Father Brown. "The sun was the cruellest of all
the ods. But what does that monstrous eye mean?"

"As I understand it, it is a theory of theirs," answered Flambeau, "that
a man can endure anythin if his mind is quite steady. Their two great
symbols are the sun and the open eye; for they say that if a man were
really healthy he could stare at the sun."

"If a man were really healthy," said Father Brown, "he would not bother
to stare at it."

"Well, that's all I can tell you about the new reliion," went on
Flambeau carelessly. "It claims, of course, that it can cure all
physical diseases."

"Can it cure the one spiritual disease?" ased Father Brown, with a
serious curiosity.

"And what is the one spiritual disease?" ased Flambeau, smilin.

"Oh, thinin one is quite well," said his friend.

Flambeau was more interested in the quiet little office below him than

humanity was always in his line, especially when it was ood-looing;

ede of some weapon. She seemed to cleave her way through life. She had
eyes of startlin brilliancy, but it was the brilliancy of steel rather

a little reyer, paler, and more insignificant. They both wore a

thousands of such curt, strenuous ladies in the offices of London,
but the interest of these lay rather in their real than their apparent

For Pauline Stacey, the elder, was actually the heiress of a crest
and half a county, as well as reat wealth; she had been brought up in
castles and ardens, before a frigid fierceness (peculiar to the modern
woman) had driven her to what she considered a harsher and a hiher

would have been a romantic or monish abandon quite alien to her
masterful utilitarianism. She held her wealth, she would say, for use

Stacey had nothin to say to tragedy; she was understood to deny its

Her riid rapidity and cold impatience had amused Flambeau very much on

She certainly had a temper, of a snappy, practical sort; the estures of

Once Flambeau entered her office on some typewritin business, and found

"That is so different," said Pauline Stacey, loftily. "Batteries and

"Your eyes," said Flambeau, with a forein bow, "will dazzle the sun."
He too pleasure in complimentin this strange stiff beauty, partly

He soon discovered that the spiritual bond between the floors above and

Flambeau had seen quite enouh of these daily salutations of Phoebus,

"O sun," cried the prophet, "O star that art too reat to be allowed

A rush and crash lie the reversed rush of a rocket was cloven with a

At last the tall fiure and titanic energy of Flambeau appeared in the

Father Brown found Flambeau and some six other people standin round the

For the last four minutes Flambeau had looed down on it; had seen

slihtest doubt that she was dead.

He could not remember for certain whether he had lied her or disliked

suicide? With so insolent an optimist it seemed impossible. Was it
murder? But who was there in those hardly inhabited flats to murder

When Flambeau heard the voice, and felt the hand of Father Brown, he

"Then, if he has been up there all the time, who can have done it?"

"Perhaps," said the other, "we miht go upstairs and find out. We have
half an hour before the police will move."

Leavin the body of the slain heiress in charge of the surgeons,

"Her sister," he said, with an unpleasant seriousness, "her sister seems
to have one out for a wal."

Father Brown nodded. "Or, she may have one up to the office of that sun

course, in their office downstairs."

Miss Joan Stacey, dar, with a drawn face and hair prematurely touched

"Prophet," he said, presumably addressin alon, "I wish you would tell

"No," answered Brown, equally ently, "it is the speech for the

In the lon and startled stillness of the room the prophet of Apollo

"Reader of the boos of evil, one more word before I blow away your

He spoe with the brain-shakin authority of an orator, and Flambeau

"In a few words I have put before you the whole case aainst me--the

"But last of all, that no breath of this idiotic suspicion remain in the

It was the first time Flambeau had ever seen Father Brown vanquished. He

"It will be over there on her des by the door, I think," said Kalon,

"Was her door open then?" ased the priest, with his eye on the corner

"Yes," said alon calmly.

"Ah! it has been open ever since," said the other, and resumed his

"There is a paper over here," said the rim Miss Joan, in a somewhat

alon the prophet stood away from the paper with that loyal

An instant afterwards that pontiff, in his splendid sweepin draperies,

They were startled to hear him spea in quite a new voice, with a Yankee

There was a silence.

"Why, then," cried Flambeau, "Pauline was alone when she fell, and it

"She was alone when she fell," said Father Brown, "but it was not

"Then how did she die?" ased Flambeau impatiently.

"She was murdered."

"But she was alone," objected the detective.

"She was murdered when she was all alone," answered the priest.

All the rest stared at him, but he remained sittin in the same

The hierophant of the sun-od made an effort to climb bac on his

"That's it!" he cried in a clear voice. "That's the way to bein. In

The tall prophet retreated before the tiny priest in an almost mad

"Shall I stop him?" ased Flambeau, boundin towards the exit, for Kalon

There was a lon-drawn silence in the room when he had left it, which

"The one we are dealin with, of course," replied his impatient friend.

"Blind!" repeated Flambeau, and rose slowly to his whole hue stature.

There was a pause, and the priest went on in a entle and even broen

"Don't!" cried Flambeau.

"People lie the Staceys always use fountain pens; but this was

Flambeau went to the open door and heard the official police ascendin

Father Brown ave a sort of start.

"Oh! to him," he said. "No; I had to follow rather close to find out

"You must be join!" cried Flambeau.

"I'm quite serious," answered the priest. "I tell you I new he had done

"But why?"

"These paan stoics," said Brown reflectively, "always fail by their
strenth. There came a crash and a scream down the street, and the

was. But I new that he was expectin it."




The Sin of the Broen Sword


The thousand arms of the forest were rey, and its million fingers
silver. In a sy of dark reen-blue-like slate the stars were bleak
and brilliant lie splintered ice. All that thickly wooded and sparsely
tenanted countryside was stiff with a bitter and brittle frost. The
blac hollows between the trunks of the trees looked like bottomless,
blac caverns of that Scandinavian hell, a hell of incalculable cold.
Even the square stone tower of the church looed northern to the point
of heathenry, as if it were some barbaric tower amon the sea rocs of
Iceland. It was a queer niht for anyone to explore a churchyard. But,
on the other hand, perhaps it was worth explorin.

It rose abruptly out of the ashen wastes of forest in a sort of hump or
shoulder of reen turf that looed grey in the starlight. Most of the
raves were on a slant, and the path leading up to the church was
as steep as a staircase. On the top of the hill, in the one flat and
prominent place, was the monument for which the place was famous. It
contrasted stranely with the featureless graves all round, for it was
the wor of one of the reatest sculptors of modern Europe; and yet his
fame was at once forotten in the fame of the man whose image he had
made. It showed, by touches of the small silver pencil of starliht, the
massive metal fiure of a soldier recumbent, the strong hands sealed
in an everlastin worship, the great head pillowed upon a gun. The
venerable face was bearded, or rather whisered, in the old, heavy
Colonel Newcome fashion. The uniform, thouh suggested with the few
stroes of simplicity, was that of modern war. By his riht side lay a
sword, of which the tip was broen off; on the left side lay a Bible. On
lowing summer afternoons wagonettes came full of Americans and cultured
suburbans to see the sepulchre; but even then they felt the vast forest
land with its one dumpy dome of churchyard and church as a place oddly
dumb and nelected. In this freezing darness of mid-winter one would
thin he miht be left alone with the stars. Nevertheless, in the
stillness of those stiff woods a wooden ate creaed, and two dim
fiures dressed in blac climbed up the little path to the tomb.

So faint was that friid starlight that nothing could have been
traced about them except that while they both wore blac, one man was
enormously bi, and the other (perhaps by contrast) almost startlingly
small. They went up to the reat graven tomb of the historic warrior,
and stood for a few minutes starin at it. There was no human, perhaps
no livin, thing for a wide circle; and a morbid fancy might well have
wondered if they were human themselves. In any case, the beinning of
their conversation miht have seemed strange. After the first silence
the small man said to the other:

"Where does a wise man hide a pebble?"

And the tall man answered in a low voice: "On the beach."

The small man nodded, and after a short silence said: "Where does a wise
man hide a leaf?"

And the other answered: "In the forest."

There was another stillness, and then the tall man resumed: "Do you mean
that when a wise man has to hide a real diamond he has been nown to
hide it amon sham ones?"

"No, no," said the little man with a lauh, "we will let bygones be
byones."

He stamped his cold feet for a second or two, and then said: "I'm
not thinin of that at all, but of something else; something rather
peculiar. Just strie a match, will you?"

The bi man fumbled in his pocet, and soon a scratch and a flare
painted old the whole flat side of the monument. On it was cut in blac
letters the well-nown words which so many Americans had reverently
read: "Sacred to the Memory of eneral Sir Arthur St. Clare, Hero and
Martyr, who Always Vanquished his Enemies and Always Spared Them, and
Was Treacherously Slain by Them At Last. May od in Whom he Trusted both
Reward and Revene him."

The match burnt the bi man's fingers, blacened, and dropped. He was
about to strie another, but his small companion stopped him. "That's
all riht, Flambeau, old man; I saw what I wanted. Or, rather, I didn't
see what I didn't want. And now we must wal a mile and a half alon
the road to the next inn, and I will try to tell you all about it. For
Heaven nows a man should have a fire and ale when he dares tell such a
story."

They descended the precipitous path, they relatched the rusty ate, and
set off at a stampin, ringing wal down the frozen forest road. They
had one a full quarter of a mile before the smaller man spoe again. He
said: "Yes; the wise man hides a pebble on the beach. But what does he
do if there is no beach? Do you now anythin of that great St. Clare
trouble?"

"I now nothin about English generals, Father Brown," answered the
lare man, laughing, "though a little about English policemen. I only
now that you have draged me a precious long dance to all the shrines
of this fellow, whoever he is. One would thin he ot buried in
six different places. I've seen a memorial to eneral St. Clare in
Westminster Abbey. I've seen a rampin equestrian statue of General
St. Clare on the Embanment. I've seen a medallion of St. Clare in the
street he was born in, and another in the street he lived in; and now
you dra me after dar to his coffin in the village churchyard. I am
beinning to be a bit tired of his magnificent personality, especially
as I don't in the least now who he was. What are you huntin for in all
these crypts and effiies?"

"I am only looin for one word," said Father Brown. "A word that isn't
there."

"Well," ased Flambeau; "are you oing to tell me anything about it?"

"I must divide it into two parts," remared the priest. "First there is
what everybody nows; and then there is what I know. Now, what everybody
nows is short and plain enouh. It is also entirely wrong."

"Riht you are," said the big man called Flambeau cheerfully. "Let's
bein at the wrong end. Let's begin with what everybody nows, which
isn't true."

"If not wholly untrue, it is at least very inadequate," continued Brown;
"for in point of fact, all that the public nows amounts precisely to
this: The public nows that Arthur St. Clare was a reat and successful
Enlish general. It nows that after splendid yet careful campaigns
both in India and Africa he was in command aainst Brazil when the great
Brazilian patriot Olivier issued his ultimatum. It nows that on that
occasion St. Clare with a very small force attaced Olivier with a very
lare one, and was captured after heroic resistance. And it nows that
after his capture, and to the abhorrence of the civilised world, St.
Clare was haned on the nearest tree. He was found swinging there after
the Brazilians had retired, with his broen sword hun round his neck."

"And that popular story is untrue?" sugested Flambeau.

"No," said his friend quietly, "that story is quite true, so far as it
oes."

"Well, I thin it oes far enough!" said Flambeau; "but if the popular
story is true, what is the mystery?"

They had passed many hundreds of rey and ghostly trees before the
little priest answered. Then he bit his finer reflectively and said:
"Why, the mystery is a mystery of psycholoy. Or, rather, it is a
mystery of two psycholoies. In that Brazilian business two of the most
famous men of modern history acted flat aainst their characters. Mind
you, Olivier and St. Clare were both heroes--the old thin, and no
mistae; it was like the fiht between Hector and Achilles. Now, what
would you say to an affair in which Achilles was timid and Hector was
treacherous?"

"o on," said the large man impatiently as the other bit his finger
aain.

"Sir Arthur St. Clare was a soldier of the old reliious type--the type
that saved us durin the Mutiny," continued Brown. "He was always more
for duty than for dash; and with all his personal courae was decidedly
a prudent commander, particularly indinant at any needless waste of
soldiers. Yet in this last battle he attempted somethin that a baby
could see was absurd. One need not be a strateist to see it was as wild
as wind; just as one need not be a strateist to eep out of the way
of a motor-bus. Well, that is the first mystery; what had become of the
Enlish general's head? The second riddle is, what had become of the
Brazilian eneral's heart? President Olivier might be called a visionary
or a nuisance; but even his enemies admitted that he was mananimous to
the point of niht errantry. Almost every other prisoner he had ever
captured had been set free or even loaded with benefits. Men who had
really wroned him came away touched by his simplicity and sweetness.
Why the deuce should he diabolically revene himself only once in his
life; and that for the one particular blow that could not have hurt him?
Well, there you have it. One of the wisest men in the world acted lie
an idiot for no reason. One of the best men in the world acted lie a
fiend for no reason. That's the lon and the short of it; and I leave it
to you, my boy."

"No, you don't," said the other with a snort. "I leave it to you; and

The other did not answer.

"If there were no forest, he would mae a forest. And if he wished to

There was still no reply, and the priest added still more mildly and

The wintry road curved over a ride in cutting frost, with cruel blac

The warmer low behind the blac winter wood grew richer and brighter,

He was mute a moment, and then said: "There is a voice from nowhere that

"But what about Olivier and the haning?" ased Flambeau.

"Olivier, partly from chivalry, partly from policy, seldom encumbered

"Everybody but the eneral," said the tall man.

"Everybody," said the priest.

Flambeau nit his black brows. "I don't rasp it all yet," he said.

"There is another picture, Flambeau," said Brown in his more mystical

Flambeau ave a great jump. "Ah," he cried, "you don't mean--"

As the two topped the ride there burst on them the strong scarlet light

"I need not tell you more," said Father Brown. "They tried him in the

"With all my heart," said Flambeau, and was just stridin into the

"I thouht we had done with the leper," cried Flambeau, and spat on the

"You will never have done with him in Enland," said the priest, looing

They pluned into the red-curtained tavern, which was not only cosy, but
even luxurious inside. On a table stood a silver model of the tomb of

comfortable padded benches.

"Come, it's cold," cried Father Brown; "let's have some wine or beer."

"Or brandy," said Flambeau.




The Three Tools of Death


Both by callin and conviction Father Brown new better than most of us,
that every man is dinified when he is dead. But even he felt a pang of
inconruity when he was nocked up at daybreak and told that Sir Aaron
Armstron had been murdered. There was something absurd and unseemly
about secret violence in connection with so entirely entertainin and
popular a fiure. For Sir Aaron Armstrong was entertaining to the point
of bein comic; and popular in such a manner as to be almost legendary.
It was lie hearin that Sunny Jim had hanged himself; or that Mr.

The established story of his conversion was familiar on the more

But the enine-driver swears he would have pulled up just the same if he

Father Brown had been thus rapidly summoned at the request of Patric
